<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Complex number</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Number with a real and an imaginary part</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_plus_bi.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="122" data-file-width="182" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/A_plus_bi.svg/250px-A_plus_bi.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/A_plus_bi.svg/375px-A_plus_bi.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/A_plus_bi.svg/500px-A_plus_bi.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_plus_bi.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A complex number can be visually represented as a pair of numbers <span class="texhtml">(<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>)</span> forming a vector on a diagram called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argand_diagram" title="Argand diagram">Argand diagram</a>, representing the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>. <i>Re</i> is the real axis, <i>Im</i> is the imaginary axis, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span> is the "<a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a>", that satisfies <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>complex number</b> is an element of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_system" title="Number system">number system</a> that extends the <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> with a specific element denoted <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span>, called the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a> and satisfying the <a href="/wiki/Equation" title="Equation">equation</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span>; every complex number can be expressed in the form <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span>, where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> are real numbers. Because no real number satisfies the above equation, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span> was called an <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_number" title="Imaginary number">imaginary number</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>. For the complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> is called the <b><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="real_part"></span><span class="vanchor-text">real part</span></span></b> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> is called the <b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023754711" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="vanchor"><span id="imaginary_part"></span><span class="vanchor-text">imaginary part</span></span></b>. The set of complex numbers is denoted by either of the symbols <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> or <span class="texhtml"><b>C</b></span>. Despite the historical nomenclature "imaginary", complex numbers are regarded in the <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_sciences" title="Mathematical sciences">mathematical sciences</a> as just as "real" as the real numbers and are fundamental in many aspects of the scientific description of the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complex numbers allow solutions to all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynomial_equation" title="Polynomial equation">polynomial equations</a>, even those that have no solutions in real numbers. More precisely, the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a> asserts that every non-constant polynomial equation with real or complex coefficients has a solution which is a complex number. For example, the equation
<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (x+1)^{2}=-9}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>9</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (x+1)^{2}=-9}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle (x+1)^{2}=-9}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f8c58fbc3ad9fa5d3c0e77886c766272117716ef" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.265ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>
has no real solution, since the square of a real number cannot be negative, but has the two nonreal complex solutions <span class="texhtml">−1 + 3<i>i</i></span> and <span class="texhtml">−1 − 3<i>i</i></span>.
</p><p>Addition, subtraction and multiplication of complex numbers can be naturally defined by using the rule <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span> combined with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associative_law" title="Associative law">associative</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutative_law" title="Commutative law">commutative</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributive_law" title="Distributive law">distributive</a> laws. Every nonzero complex number has a <a href="/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse" title="Multiplicative inverse">multiplicative inverse</a>. This makes the complex numbers a <a href="/wiki/Field_(mathematics)" title="Field (mathematics)">field</a> that has the real numbers as a subfield. The complex numbers also form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_vector_space" title="Real vector space">real vector space</a> of dimension two, with <span class="texhtml">{1, <i>i</i>}</span> as a <a href="/wiki/Standard_basis" title="Standard basis">standard basis</a>.
</p><p>This standard basis makes the complex numbers a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_plane" title="Cartesian plane">Cartesian plane</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>. This allows a geometric interpretation of the complex numbers and their operations, and conversely expressing in terms of complex numbers some geometric properties and constructions. For example, the real numbers form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_line" title="Real line">real line</a> which is identified to the horizontal axis of the complex plane. The complex numbers of <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">absolute value</a> one form the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a>. The addition of a complex number is a <a href="/wiki/Translation_(geometry)" title="Translation (geometry)">translation</a> in the complex plane, and the multiplication by a complex number is a <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similarity</a> centered at the origin. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_conjugation" title="Complex conjugation">complex conjugation</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Reflection_symmetry" title="Reflection symmetry">reflection symmetry</a> with respect to the real axis. The complex absolute value is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_norm" title="Euclidean norm">Euclidean norm</a>.
</p><p>In summary, the complex numbers form a rich structure that is simultaneously an <a href="/wiki/Algebraically_closed_field" title="Algebraically closed field">algebraically closed field</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutative_algebra_(structure)" title="Commutative algebra (structure)">commutative algebra</a> over the reals, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector_space" title="Euclidean vector space">Euclidean vector space</a> of dimension two.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="832" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg/230px-Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg/345px-Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg/460px-Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_a_complex_number.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = <i>x</i> + <i>iy</i></span> on the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>. The real part is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span>, and its imaginary part is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">y</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>A complex number is a number of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span>, where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_numbers" title="Real numbers">real numbers</a>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> is an indeterminate satisfying <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span>. For example, <span class="texhtml">2 + 3<i>i</i></span> is a complex number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>This way, a complex number is defined as a <a href="/wiki/Polynomial" title="Polynomial">polynomial</a> with real coefficients in the single indeterminate <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span>, for which the relation <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> + 1 = 0</span> is imposed. Based on this definition, complex numbers can be added and multiplied, using the addition and multiplication for polynomials. The relation <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> + 1 = 0</span> induces the equalities <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>4<i>k</i></sup> = 1, <i>i</i><sup>4<i>k</i>+1</sup> = <i>i</i>, <i>i</i><sup>4<i>k</i>+2</sup> = −1,</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>4<i>k</i>+3</sup> = −<i>i</i>,</span> which hold for all integers <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">k</span>; these allow the reduction of any polynomial that results from the addition and multiplication of complex numbers to a linear polynomial in <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span>, again of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span> with real coefficients <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a, b.</span>
</p><p>The real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> is called the <i>real part</i> of the complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span>; the real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> is called its <i>imaginary part</i>. To emphasize, the imaginary part does not include a factor <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span>; that is, the imaginary part is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span>, not <span class="texhtml"><i>bi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Formally, the complex numbers are defined as the <a href="/wiki/Quotient_ring" title="Quotient ring">quotient ring</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Polynomial_ring" title="Polynomial ring">polynomial ring</a> in the indeterminate <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span>, by the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory)" title="Ideal (ring theory)">ideal</a> generated by the polynomial <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> + 1</span> (see <a href="#Construction_as_a_quotient_field">below</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourbaki_1998_loc=§VIII.1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bourbaki_1998_loc=§VIII.1-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation">Notation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Notation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> can be regarded as a complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + 0<i>i</i></span>, whose imaginary part is 0. A purely <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_number" title="Imaginary number">imaginary number</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>bi</i></span> is a complex number <span class="texhtml">0 + <i>bi</i></span>, whose real part is zero. As with polynomials, it is common to write <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> for <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + 0<i>i</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>bi</i></span> for <span class="texhtml">0 + <i>bi</i></span>. Moreover, when the imaginary part is negative, that is, <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i> = −<i>|b|</i> &lt; 0</span>, it is common to write <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> − <i>|b|i</i></span> instead of <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + (−<i>|b|</i>)<i>i</i></span>; for example, for <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i> = −4</span>, <span class="texhtml">3 − 4<i>i</i></span> can be written instead of <span class="texhtml">3 + (−4)<i>i</i></span>.
</p><p>Since the multiplication of the indeterminate <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> and a real is commutative in polynomials with real coefficients, the polynomial <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span> may be written as <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>ib</i>.</span> This is often expedient for imaginary parts denoted by expressions, for example, when <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> is a radical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhlfors1979_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhlfors1979-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The real part of a complex number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> is denoted by <span class="texhtml">Re(<i>z</i>)</span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\mathcal {Re}}(z)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">R</mi>
<mi class="MJX-tex-caligraphic" mathvariant="script">e</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>

</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set (mathematics)">set</a> of all complex numbers is denoted by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb{C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> (<a href="/wiki/Blackboard_bold" title="Blackboard bold">blackboard bold</a>) or <span class="texhtml"><b>C</b></span> (upright bold).
</p><p>In some disciplines, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">j</span> is used instead of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span> as <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span> is frequently used to represent <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> In these cases, complex numbers are written as <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bj</i></span>, or <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>jb</i></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Visualization">Visualization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Visualization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">Complex plane</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Complex_number_illustration.svg/220px-Complex_number_illustration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Complex_number_illustration.svg/330px-Complex_number_illustration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Complex_number_illustration.svg/440px-Complex_number_illustration.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A complex number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span>, as a point (black) and its position vector (blue)</div></div></div>
<p>A complex number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> can thus be identified with an <a href="/wiki/Ordered_pair" title="Ordered pair">ordered pair</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (\Re (z),\Im (z))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">ℜ<!-- ℜ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">ℑ<!-- ℑ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (\Re (z),\Im (z))}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle (\Re (z),\Im (z))}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbf496ff70298f00e695abe890c7ede154eb0a89" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.85ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of real numbers, which in turn may be interpreted as coordinates of a point in a two-dimensional space. The most immediate space is the Euclidean plane with suitable coordinates, which is then called <i>complex plane</i> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argand_diagram" title="Argand diagram">Argand diagram</a>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-12">[10]</a></sup> named after <a href="/wiki/Jean-Robert_Argand" title="Jean-Robert Argand">Jean-Robert Argand</a>. Another prominent space on which the coordinates may be projected is the two-dimensional surface of a sphere, which is then called <a href="/wiki/Riemann_sphere" title="Riemann sphere">Riemann sphere</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartesian_complex_plane">Cartesian complex plane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cartesian complex plane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The definition of the complex numbers involving two arbitrary real values immediately suggests the use of Cartesian coordinates in the complex plane. The horizontal (<i>real</i>) axis is generally used to display the real part, with increasing values to the right, and the imaginary part marks the vertical (<i>imaginary</i>) axis, with increasing values upwards.
</p><p>A charted number may be viewed either as the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/coordinatize" title="wikt:coordinatize">coordinatized</a> point or as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" title="Vector (geometric)">position vector</a> from the origin to this point. The coordinate values of a complex number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> can hence be expressed in its <i>Cartesian</i>, <i>rectangular</i>, or <i>algebraic</i> form.
</p><p>Notably, the operations of addition and multiplication take on a very natural geometric character, when complex numbers are viewed as position vectors: addition corresponds to <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector#Addition_and_subtraction" title="Euclidean vector">vector addition</a>, while multiplication (see <a href="#Multiplication_and_division_in_polar_form">below</a>) corresponds to multiplying their magnitudes and adding the angles they make with the real axis. Viewed in this way, the multiplication of a complex number by <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> corresponds to rotating the position vector <a href="/wiki/Orientation_(geometry)" title="Orientation (geometry)">counterclockwise</a> by a quarter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turn_(geometry)" title="Turn (geometry)">turn</a> (<a href="/wiki/Right_angle" title="Right angle">90°</a>) about the origin—a fact which can be expressed algebraically as follows:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (a+bi)\cdot i=ai+b(i)^{2}=-b+ai.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_complex_plane">Polar complex plane <span class="anchor" id="Polar_form"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Polar complex plane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system" title="Polar coordinate system">Polar coordinate system</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Polar form" redirects here. For the higher-dimensional analogue, see <a href="/wiki/Polar_decomposition" title="Polar decomposition">Polar decomposition</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg/220px-Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg/330px-Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg/440px-Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex_number_illustration_modarg.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Argument <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span> and modulus <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> locate a point in the complex plane.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modulus_and_argument">Modulus and argument</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modulus and argument">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An alternative option for coordinates in the complex plane is the <a href="/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system" title="Polar coordinate system">polar coordinate system</a> that uses the distance of the point <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> from the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">O</span>), and the angle subtended between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_real_axis" title="Positive real axis">positive real axis</a> and the line segment <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Oz</span> in a counterclockwise sense. This leads to the <b>polar form</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle z=re^{i\varphi }=r(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>r</mi>
<msup>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
</mrow>

<mo>=</mo>
<mi>r</mi>

<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle z=re^{i\varphi }=r(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi )}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle z=re^{i\varphi }=r(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi )}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9956a248cde14fe13d040c6983c3a2c7c437eb2" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:27.961ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>of a complex number, where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">absolute value</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \varphi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \varphi }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\varphi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33ee699558d09cf9d653f6351f9fda0b2f4aaa3e" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.52ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Argument_(complex_analysis)" title="Argument (complex analysis)">argument</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span>.
</p><p>The <i>absolute value</i> (or <i>modulus</i> or <i>magnitude</i>) of a complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = <i>x</i> + <i>yi</i></span> is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEApostol198118_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEApostol198118-13">[11]</a></sup>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r=|z|={\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>z</mi>

</mrow>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r=|z|={\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle r=|z|={\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b59629c801aa0ddcdf17ee489e028fb9f8d4ea75" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:20.037ex; height:4.843ex;"/></div>
If <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> is a real number (that is, if <span class="texhtml"><i>y</i> = 0</span>), then <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i> = |<i>x</i>|</span>. That is, the absolute value of a real number equals its absolute value as a complex number.
</p><p>By <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagoras%27_theorem" title="Pythagoras' theorem">Pythagoras' theorem</a>, the absolute value of a complex number is the distance to the origin of the point representing the complex number in the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>.
</p><p>The <i>argument</i> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> (in many applications referred to as the "phase" <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-12">[10]</a></sup> is the angle of the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Oz</span> with the positive real axis, and is written as <span class="texhtml">arg <i>z</i></span>. As with the modulus, the argument can be found from the rectangular form <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x + yi</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>—by applying the inverse tangent to the quotient of imaginary-by-real parts. By using a half-angle identity, a single branch of the arctan suffices to cover the range <span class="texhtml">(−<i>π</i>, <i>π</i>]</span> of the <span class="texhtml">arg</span>-function, and avoids a more subtle case-by-case analysis
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \varphi =\arg(x+yi)={\begin{cases}2\arctan \left({\dfrac {y}{{\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}+x}}\right)&amp;{\text{if }}y\neq 0{\text{ or }}x&gt;0,\\\pi &amp;{\text{if }}x&lt;0{\text{ and }}y=0,\\{\text{undefined}}&amp;{\text{if }}x=0{\text{ and }}y=0.\end{cases}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \varphi =\arg(x+yi)={\begin{cases}2\arctan \left({\dfrac {y}{{\sqrt {x^{2}+y^{2}}}+x}}\right)&amp;{\text{if }}y\neq 0{\text{ or }}x&gt;0,\\\pi &amp;{\text{if }}x&lt;0{\text{ and }}y=0,\\{\text{undefined}}&amp;{\text{if }}x=0{\text{ and }}y=0.\end{cases}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aca1d2fbb3d430f1595bf59ea0d7ad156a88ec14" style="vertical-align: -6.005ex; width:68.999ex; height:13.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Normally, as given above, the <a href="/wiki/Principal_value" title="Principal value">principal value</a> in the interval <span class="texhtml">(−<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>]</span> is chosen. If the arg value is negative, values in the range <span class="texhtml">(−<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>]</span> or <span class="texhtml">[0, 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>)</span> can be obtained by adding <span class="texhtml">2<i>π</i></span>. The value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span> is expressed in <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a> in this article. It can increase by any integer multiple of <span class="texhtml">2<i>π</i></span> and still give the same angle, viewed as subtended by the rays of the positive real axis and from the origin through <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span>. Hence, the arg function is sometimes considered as <a href="/wiki/Multivalued_function" title="Multivalued function">multivalued</a>. The polar angle for the complex number 0 is indeterminate, but arbitrary choice of the polar angle 0 is common.
</p><p>The value of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span> equals the result of <a href="/wiki/Atan2" title="Atan2">atan2</a>:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \varphi =\operatorname {atan2} \left(\operatorname {Im} (z),\operatorname {Re} (z)\right).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \varphi =\operatorname {atan2} \left(\operatorname {Im} (z),\operatorname {Re} (z)\right).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \varphi =\operatorname {atan2} \left(\operatorname {Im} (z),\operatorname {Re} (z)\right).}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a49a1a0a0268b3959b89d68153854d67d0461911" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:25.107ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Together, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span> give another way of representing complex numbers, the <i>polar form</i>, as the combination of modulus and argument fully specify the position of a point on the plane. Recovering the original rectangular co-ordinates from the polar form is done by the formula called <i>trigonometric form</i>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle z=r(\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi ).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_graphs">Complex graphs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Complex graphs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domain_coloring" title="Domain coloring">Domain coloring</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex-plot.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="579" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Complex-plot.png/220px-Complex-plot.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Complex-plot.png/330px-Complex-plot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Complex-plot.png/440px-Complex-plot.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex-plot.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A color wheel graph of the expression <span class="texhtml"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">(<i>z</i><sup>2</sup> − 1)(<i>z</i> − 2 − <i>i</i>)<sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><i>z</i><sup>2</sup> + 2 + 2<i>i</i></span></span></span></div></div></div>
<p>When visualizing <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex functions</a>, both a complex input and output are needed. Because each complex number is represented in two dimensions, visually graphing a complex function would require the perception of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_dimensional_space" title="Four dimensional space">four dimensional space</a>, which is possible only in projections. Because of this, other ways of visualizing complex functions have been designed.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Domain_coloring" title="Domain coloring">domain coloring</a> the output dimensions are represented by color and brightness, respectively. Each point in the complex plane as domain is <i>ornated</i>, typically with <i>color</i> representing the argument of the complex number, and <i>brightness</i> representing the magnitude. Dark spots mark moduli near zero, brighter spots are farther away from the origin, the gradation may be discontinuous, but is assumed as monotonous. The colors often vary in steps of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span> for <span class="texhtml">0</span> to <span class="texhtml">2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span> from red, yellow, green, cyan, blue, to magenta. These plots are called <a href="/wiki/Domain_coloring" title="Domain coloring">color wheel graphs</a>. This provides a simple way to visualize the functions without losing information. The picture shows zeros for <span class="texhtml">±1, (2 + <i>i</i>)</span> and poles at <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \pm {\sqrt {-2-2i}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>±<!-- ± --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>i</mi>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pm {\sqrt {-2-2i}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \pm {\sqrt {-2-2i}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61bc7e5f92a9bc9585b7db872d44fd3cb7fb9665" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.167ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Negative_number#History" title="Negative number">Negative number § History</a></div>
<p>The solution in <a href="/wiki/Nth_root" title="Nth root">radicals</a> (without <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">trigonometric functions</a>) of a general <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic equation</a>, when all three of its roots are real numbers, contains the square roots of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_numbers" title="Negative numbers">negative numbers</a>, a situation that cannot be rectified by factoring aided by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rational_root_test" title="Rational root test">rational root test</a>, if the cubic is <a href="/wiki/Irreducible_polynomial" title="Irreducible polynomial">irreducible</a>; this is the so-called  <i><a href="/wiki/Casus_irreducibilis" title="Casus irreducibilis">casus irreducibilis</a></i> ("irreducible case"). This conundrum led Italian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Gerolamo_Cardano" title="Gerolamo Cardano">Gerolamo Cardano</a> to conceive of complex numbers in around 1545 in his <i>Ars Magna</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> though his understanding was rudimentary; moreover he later dismissed complex numbers as "subtle as they are useless".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Work on the problem of general polynomials ultimately led to the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a>, which shows that with complex numbers, a solution exists to every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polynomial_equation" title="Polynomial equation">polynomial equation</a> of degree one or higher. Complex numbers thus form an <a href="/wiki/Algebraically_closed_field" title="Algebraically closed field">algebraically closed field</a>, where any polynomial equation has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">root</a>.
</p><p>Many mathematicians contributed to the development of complex numbers. The rules for addition, subtraction, multiplication, and root extraction of complex numbers were developed by the Italian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Bombelli" title="Rafael Bombelli">Rafael Bombelli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> A more abstract formalism for the complex numbers was further developed by the Irish mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Rowan Hamilton</a>, who extended this abstraction to the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaternions" title="Quaternions">quaternions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest fleeting reference to <a href="/wiki/Square_root" title="Square root">square roots</a> of <a href="/wiki/Negative_number" title="Negative number">negative numbers</a> can perhaps be said to occur in the work of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_mathematics" title="Hellenistic mathematics">Greek mathematician</a> <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Hero of Alexandria</a> in the 1st century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AD" title="AD">AD</a>, where in his <i><a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria#Bibliography" title="Hero of Alexandria">Stereometrica</a></i> he considered, apparently in error, the volume of an impossible <a href="/wiki/Frustum" title="Frustum">frustum</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a> to arrive at the term <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {81-144}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mn>81</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>144</mn>

Negative quantities were not conceived of in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_mathematics" title="Hellenistic mathematics">Hellenistic mathematics</a> and Hero merely replaced it by its positive <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {144-81}}=3{\sqrt {7}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {144-81}}=3{\sqrt {7}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5fc15d48ccea867a37beef8358473f0c240dddf1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.595ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The impetus to study complex numbers as a topic in itself first arose in the 16th century when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algebraic_solution" title="Algebraic solution">algebraic solutions</a> for the roots of <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quartic_equation" title="Quartic equation">quartic</a> <a href="/wiki/Polynomial" title="Polynomial">polynomials</a> were discovered by Italian mathematicians (see <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Fontana_Tartaglia" title="Niccolò Fontana Tartaglia">Niccolò Fontana Tartaglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerolamo_Cardano" title="Gerolamo Cardano">Gerolamo Cardano</a>). It was soon realized (but proved much later)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casus_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casus-21">[19]</a></sup> that these formulas, even if one was interested only in real solutions, sometimes required the manipulation of square roots of negative numbers. As an example, Tartaglia's formula for a cubic equation of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i><sup>3</sup> = <i>px</i> + <i>q</i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[c]</a></sup> gives the solution to the equation <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i><sup>3</sup> = <i>x</i></span> as
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}\left(\left({\sqrt {-1}}\right)^{1/3}+\left({\sqrt {-1}}\right)^{-1/3}\right).}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

However, formal calculations with complex numbers show that the equation <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sup>3</sup> = <i>i</i></span> has three solutions: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle -i,{\frac {{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}},{\frac {-{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -i,{\frac {{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}},{\frac {-{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle -i,{\frac {{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}},{\frac {-{\sqrt {3}}+i}{2}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86b9421c6b43d0170f7dd71fcb4b84a276c6ae40" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:22.288ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span> Substituting these in turn for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}^{1/3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}^{1/3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}^{1/3}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c98e4b5e6efdde9ca68f092329a81c1e00234a64" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.605ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span> in Tartaglia's cubic formula and simplifying, one gets 0, 1 and −1 as the solutions of <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i><sup>3</sup> − <i>x</i> = 0</span>. Of course this particular equation can be solved at sight but it does illustrate that when general formulas are used to solve cubic equations with real roots then, as later mathematicians showed rigorously,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[d]</a></sup> the use of complex numbers <a href="/wiki/Casus_irreducibilis" title="Casus irreducibilis">is unavoidable</a>. Rafael Bombelli was the first to address explicitly these seemingly paradoxical solutions of cubic equations and developed the rules for complex arithmetic trying to resolve these issues.
</p><p>The term "imaginary" for these quantities was coined by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> in 1637, who was at pains to stress their unreal nature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... sometimes only imaginary, that is one can imagine as many as I said in each equation, but sometimes there exists no quantity that matches that which we imagine.<br/>
[<i>... quelquefois seulement imaginaires c'est-à-dire que l'on peut toujours en imaginer autant que j'ai dit en chaque équation, mais qu'il n'y a quelquefois aucune quantité qui corresponde à celle qu'on imagine.</i>]</p></blockquote>
<p>A further source of confusion was that the equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}^{2}={\sqrt {-1}}{\sqrt {-1}}=-1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<mo>=</mo>

The incorrect use of this identity (and the related identity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle {\frac {1}{\sqrt {a}}}={\sqrt {\frac {1}{a}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This difficulty eventually led to the convention of using the special symbol <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> in place of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {-1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ea1ea9ac61e6e1e84ac39130f78143c18865719" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.906ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> to guard against this mistake.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Even so, Euler considered it natural to introduce students to complex numbers much earlier than we do today. In his elementary algebra text book, <i><a href="/wiki/Elements_of_Algebra" title="Elements of Algebra">Elements of Algebra</a></i>, he introduces these numbers almost at once and then uses them in a natural way throughout.
</p><p>In the 18th century complex numbers gained wider use, as it was noticed that formal manipulation of complex expressions could be used to simplify calculations involving trigonometric functions. For instance, in 1730 <a href="/wiki/Abraham_de_Moivre" title="Abraham de Moivre">Abraham de Moivre</a> noted that the complicated identities relating trigonometric functions of an integer multiple of an angle to powers of trigonometric functions of that angle could be simply re-expressed by the following well-known formula which bears his name, <a href="/wiki/De_Moivre%27s_formula" title="De Moivre's formula">de Moivre's formula</a>:
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (\cos \theta +i\sin \theta )^{n}=\cos n\theta +i\sin n\theta .}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>

<mo>+</mo>

<mo>.</mo>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (\cos \theta +i\sin \theta )^{n}=\cos n\theta +i\sin n\theta .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle (\cos \theta +i\sin \theta )^{n}=\cos n\theta +i\sin n\theta .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c100a9d6c15a5c191d1de4330644da02c4bc7ee4" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:35.466ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>In 1748, Euler went further and obtained <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler's formula">Euler's formula</a> of <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \cos \theta +i\sin \theta =e^{i\theta }}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>i</mi>

<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \cos \theta +i\sin \theta =e^{i\theta }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \cos \theta +i\sin \theta =e^{i\theta }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7374fdde62826dbddd192da0534e3d87decdcacd" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:18.704ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>by formally manipulating complex <a href="/wiki/Power_series" title="Power series">power series</a> and observed that this formula could be used to reduce any trigonometric identity to much simpler exponential identities.
</p><p>The idea of a complex number as a point in the complex plane (<a href="#Complex_plane">above</a>) was first described by <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Danish</a>–<a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Wessel" title="Caspar Wessel">Caspar Wessel</a> in 1799,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup> although it had been anticipated as early as 1685 in <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">Wallis's</a> <i>A Treatise of Algebra</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wessel's memoir appeared in the Proceedings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Academy" title="Copenhagen Academy">Copenhagen Academy</a> but went largely unnoticed. In 1806 <a href="/wiki/Jean-Robert_Argand" title="Jean-Robert Argand">Jean-Robert Argand</a> independently issued a pamphlet on complex numbers and provided a rigorous proof of the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra#History" title="Fundamental theorem of algebra">fundamental theorem of algebra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a> had earlier published an essentially <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topological</a> proof of the theorem in 1797 but expressed his doubts at the time about "the true metaphysics of the square root of −1".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> It was not until 1831 that he overcame these doubts and published his treatise on complex numbers as points in the plane,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ewald_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ewald-30">[26]</a></sup> largely establishing modern notation and terminology:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGauss1831638_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGauss1831638-31">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>If one formerly contemplated this subject from a false point of view and therefore found a mysterious darkness, this is in large part attributable to clumsy terminology. Had one not called +1, -1, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {-1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ea1ea9ac61e6e1e84ac39130f78143c18865719" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.906ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> positive, negative, or imaginary (or even impossible) units, but instead, say, direct, inverse, or lateral units, then there could scarcely have been talk of such darkness.</p></blockquote>
<p>In the beginning of the 19th century, other mathematicians discovered independently the geometrical representation of the complex numbers: Buée,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._V._Mourey" title="C. V. Mourey">Mourey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_Warren_(mathematician)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John Warren (mathematician) (page does not exist)">Warren</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Fran%C3%A7ais" title="Jacques Frédéric Français">Français</a> and his brother, <a href="/wiki/Giusto_Bellavitis" title="Giusto Bellavitis">Bellavitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The English mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.H._Hardy" title="G.H. Hardy">G.H. Hardy</a> remarked that Gauss was the first mathematician to use complex numbers in 'a really confident and scientific way' although mathematicians such as <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Henrik_Abel" title="Niels Henrik Abel">Niels Henrik Abel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gustav_Jacob_Jacobi" title="Carl Gustav Jacob Jacobi">Carl Gustav Jacob Jacobi</a> were necessarily using them routinely before Gauss published his 1831 treatise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Augustin-Louis_Cauchy" title="Augustin-Louis Cauchy">Augustin-Louis Cauchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a> together brought the fundamental ideas of <a href="#Complex_analysis">complex analysis</a> to a high state of completion, commencing around 1825 in Cauchy's case.
</p><p>The common terms used in the theory are chiefly due to the founders. Argand called <span class="texhtml">cos <i>φ</i> + <i>i</i> sin <i>φ</i></span> the <i>direction factor</i>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r={\sqrt {a^{2}+b^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>r</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<msup>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<msup>
<mi>b</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r={\sqrt {a^{2}+b^{2}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle r={\sqrt {a^{2}+b^{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c06427751d7f71ba70ddfae47fb47e6386324ae6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.647ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span> the <i>modulus</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[e]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> Cauchy (1821) called <span class="texhtml">cos <i>φ</i> + <i>i</i> sin <i>φ</i></span> the <i>reduced form</i> (l'expression réduite)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> and apparently introduced the term <i>argument</i>; Gauss used <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {-1}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\sqrt {-1}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ea1ea9ac61e6e1e84ac39130f78143c18865719" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.906ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[f]</a></sup> introduced the term <i>complex number</i> for <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[g]</a></sup> and called <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span> the <i>norm</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[h]</a></sup> The expression <i>direction coefficient</i>, often used for <span class="texhtml">cos <i>φ</i> + <i>i</i> sin <i>φ</i></span>, is due to Hankel (1867),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[42]</a></sup> and <i>absolute value,</i> for <i>modulus,</i> is due to Weierstrass.
</p><p>Later classical writers on the general theory include <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dedekind" title="Richard Dedekind">Richard Dedekind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_H%C3%B6lder" title="Otto Hölder">Otto Hölder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">Henri Poincaré</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Schwarz" title="Hermann Schwarz">Hermann Schwarz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Weierstrass" title="Karl Weierstrass">Karl Weierstrass</a> and many others. Important work (including a systematization) in complex multivariate calculus has been started at beginning of the 20th century. Important results have been achieved by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wirtinger" title="Wilhelm Wirtinger">Wilhelm Wirtinger</a> in 1927.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_and_operations">Relations and operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Relations and operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equality">Equality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Equality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Complex numbers have a similar definition of equality to real numbers; two complex numbers <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sub>1</sub> + <i>b</i><sub>1</sub><i>i</i></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sub>2</sub> + <i>b</i><sub>2</sub><i>i</i></span> are equal <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> both their real and imaginary parts are equal, that is, if <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>a</i><sub>2</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>b</i><sub>2</sub></span>. Nonzero complex numbers written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_form" title="Polar form">polar form</a> are equal if and only if they have the same magnitude and their arguments differ by an integer multiple of <span class="texhtml">2<i>π</i></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordering">Ordering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ordering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike the real numbers, there is no natural ordering of the complex numbers. In particular, there is no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_ordering" title="Linear ordering">linear ordering</a> on the complex numbers that is compatible with addition and multiplication. Hence, the complex numbers do not have the structure of an ordered field. One explanation for this is that every non-trivial sum of squares in an <a href="/wiki/Ordered_field#nontrivialSquareSum" title="Ordered field">ordered field</a> is nonzero, and <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> + 1<sup>2</sup> = 0</span> is a non-trivial sum of squares. Thus, complex numbers are naturally thought of as existing on a two-dimensional plane.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conjugate">Conjugate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conjugate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate" title="Complex conjugate">Complex conjugate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Complex_conjugate_picture.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Complex_conjugate_picture.svg/180px-Complex_conjugate_picture.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Complex_conjugate_picture.svg/270px-Complex_conjugate_picture.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Complex_conjugate_picture.svg/360px-Complex_conjugate_picture.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Complex_conjugate_picture.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geometric representation of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> and its conjugate <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><span style="text-decoration:overline;">z</span></span> in the complex plane</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_conjugate" title="Complex conjugate">complex conjugate</a></i> of the complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = <i>x</i> + <i>yi</i></span> is given by <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> − <i>yi</i></span>. It is denoted by either <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><span style="text-decoration:overline;">z</span></span> or <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i>*</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[43]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Unary_operation" title="Unary operation">unary operation</a> on complex numbers cannot be expressed by applying only their basic operations addition, subtraction, multiplication and division.
</p><p>Geometrically, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;"><span style="text-decoration:overline;">z</span></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Reflection_symmetry" title="Reflection symmetry">"reflection"</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> about the real axis. Conjugating twice gives the original complex number

<semantics>

</p><p>which makes this operation an <a href="/wiki/Involution_(mathematics)" title="Involution (mathematics)">involution</a>. The reflection leaves both the real part and the magnitude of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> unchanged, that is

</p><p>The product of a complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = <i>x</i> + <i>yi</i></span> and its conjugate is known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_square" title="Absolute square">absolute square</a></i>. It is always a non-negative real number and equals the square of the magnitude of each:

</p><p>This property can be used to convert a fraction with a complex denominator to an equivalent fraction with a real denominator by expanding both numerator and denominator of the fraction by the conjugate of the given denominator. This process is sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Rationalisation_(mathematics)" title="Rationalisation (mathematics)">rationalization</a>" of the denominator (although the denominator in the final expression might be an irrational real number), because it resembles the method to remove roots from simple expressions in a denominator.

Moreover, a complex number is real if and only if it equals its own conjugate.

</p><p>Conjugation is also employed in <a href="/wiki/Inversive_geometry" title="Inversive geometry">inversive geometry</a>, a branch of geometry studying reflections more general than ones about a line. In the <a href="/wiki/Network_analysis_(electrical_circuits)" title="Network analysis (electrical circuits)">network analysis of electrical circuits</a>, the complex conjugate is used in finding the equivalent impedance when the <a href="/wiki/Maximum_power_transfer_theorem" title="Maximum power transfer theorem">maximum power transfer theorem</a> is looked for.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Addition_and_subtraction">Addition and subtraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Addition and subtraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vector_Addition.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Vector_Addition.svg/220px-Vector_Addition.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Vector_Addition.svg/330px-Vector_Addition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Vector_Addition.svg/440px-Vector_Addition.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vector_Addition.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Addition of two complex numbers can be done geometrically by constructing a parallelogram.</div></div></div>
<p>Two complex numbers <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a=x+yi}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle b=u+vi}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8124f0619fed742fa17b7b1c1574f35eb2eb47a7" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:10.196ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> are most easily <a href="/wiki/Addition" title="Addition">added</a> by separately adding their real and imaginary parts. That is to say:
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a+b=(x+yi)+(u+vi)=(x+u)+(y+v)i.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Subtraction" title="Subtraction">subtraction</a> can be performed as

</p><p>Multiplication of a complex number <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a=x+yi}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>+</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mi>i</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a=x+yi}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle a=x+yi}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/849881914f6464bb3c697317311c77a500f6132a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:10.456ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and a real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> can be done similarly by multiplying separately <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> and the real and imaginary parts of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle ra=r(x+yi)=rx+ryi.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle ra=r(x+yi)=rx+ryi.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle ra=r(x+yi)=rx+ryi.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cfb3c3c136f236014d4d3cce02b39b9e7ab105ca" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:26.334ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
In particular, subtraction can be done by negating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtrahend" title="Subtrahend">subtrahend</a> (that is multiplying it with <span class="texhtml">–1</span>) and adding the result to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minuend" title="Minuend">minuend</a>:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a-b=a+(-1)\,b.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
