<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mo>⋯<!-- ⋯ --></mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>X</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<msub>
<mi>a</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a_{n}X^{n}+\dotsb +a_{1}X+a_{0},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle a_{n}X^{n}+\dotsb +a_{1}X+a_{0},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3558a6da3a54245fc82ca46cbb60e6f2787fe4fd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:24.103ex; height:2.676ex;"/></div>
where the <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i><sub>0</sub>, ..., <i>a</i><sub><i>n</i></sub></span> are real numbers. The usual addition and multiplication of polynomials endows the set <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi>X</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/16d740527b0b7f949b4bf9c9ce004134bb490b68" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.952ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of all such polynomials with a <a href="/wiki/Ring_(mathematics)" title="Ring (mathematics)">ring</a> structure. This ring is called the <a href="/wiki/Polynomial_ring" title="Polynomial ring">polynomial ring</a> over the real numbers.
</p><p>The set of complex numbers is defined as the <a href="/wiki/Quotient_ring" title="Quotient ring">quotient ring</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi>X</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msup>
<mi>X</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1).}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c2d5e66358adeeb47fc3dce55f79c523e9798b03" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:15.624ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourbaki_1998_loc=§VIII.1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bourbaki_1998_loc=§VIII.1-7">[6]</a></sup> This extension field contains two square roots of <span class="texhtml">−1</span>, namely (the <a href="/wiki/Coset" title="Coset">cosets</a> of) <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> and <span class="texhtml">−<i>X</i></span>, respectively. (The cosets of) <span class="texhtml">1</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i></span> form a basis of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi>X</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msup>
<mi>X</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X]/(X^{2}+1)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4a9561fb97d235fa5d9d975ea50b9ac958058410" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.977ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> as a real <a href="/wiki/Vector_space" title="Vector space">vector space</a>, which means that each element of the extension field can be uniquely written as a <a href="/wiki/Linear_combination" title="Linear combination">linear combination</a> in these two elements. Equivalently, elements of the extension field can be written as ordered pairs <span class="texhtml">(<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>)</span> of real numbers.  The quotient ring is a field, because <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i><sup>2</sup> + 1</span> is <a href="/wiki/Irreducible_polynomial" title="Irreducible polynomial">irreducible</a> over <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0522388d36b55de7babe4bbfc49475eaf590c2bd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.325ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> so the ideal it generates is <a href="/wiki/Maximal_ideal" title="Maximal ideal">maximal</a>.
</p><p>The formulas for addition and multiplication in the ring <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X],}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi>X</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X],}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} [X],}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/44b5607f4e6eded005f2fbf81c70cfff7f26fb26" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.599ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> modulo the relation <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span>, correspond to the formulas for addition and multiplication of complex numbers defined as ordered pairs.  So the two definitions of the field <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb{C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> are <a href="/wiki/Isomorphism" title="Isomorphism">isomorphic</a> (as fields).
</p><p>Accepting that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb{C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is algebraically closed, since it is an <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_extension" title="Algebraic extension">algebraic extension</a> of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrix_representation_of_complex_numbers">Matrix representation of complex numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Matrix representation of complex numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Complex numbers <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>bi</i></span> can also be represented by <span class="texhtml">2 × 2</span> <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrices</a> that have the form:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>a</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>a</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/874762a513e504f9a2b5854c093217705dc57b43" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:10.531ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
Here the entries <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> are real numbers. As the sum and product of two such matrices is again of this form, these matrices form a <a href="/wiki/Subring" title="Subring">subring</a> of the ring <span class="texhtml">2 × 2</span> matrices.
</p><p>A simple computation shows that the map:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a+ib\mapsto {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">↦<!-- ↦ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>a</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
<mtr>
<mtd>
<mi>b</mi>
</mtd>
<mtd>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>a</mi>
</mtd>
</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a+ib\mapsto {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle a+ib\mapsto {\begin{pmatrix}a&amp;-b\\b&amp;\;\;a\end{pmatrix}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bfb2a42d93e6c0c6dc4fd84d2c534d1ccd736bf1" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:20.015ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring_isomorphism" title="Ring isomorphism">ring isomorphism</a> from the field of complex numbers to the ring of these matrices. This isomorphism associates the square of the absolute value of a complex number with the <a href="/wiki/Determinant" title="Determinant">determinant</a> of the corresponding matrix, and the conjugate of a complex number with the <a href="/wiki/Transpose" title="Transpose">transpose</a> of the matrix.
</p><p>The geometric description of the multiplication of complex numbers can also be expressed in terms of <a href="/wiki/Rotation_matrix" title="Rotation matrix">rotation matrices</a> by using this correspondence between complex numbers and such matrices. The action of the matrix on a vector <span class="texhtml">(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>)</span> corresponds to the multiplication of <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> + <i>iy</i></span> by <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> + <i>ib</i></span>. In particular, if the determinant is <span class="texhtml">1</span>, there is a real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">t</span> such that the matrix has the form:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}\cos t&amp;-\sin t\\\sin t&amp;\;\;\cos t\end{pmatrix}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">
<mtr>
<mtd>

<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="thickmathspace"></mspace>

<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>

</mtr>
</mtable>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}\cos t&amp;-\sin t\\\sin t&amp;\;\;\cos t\end{pmatrix}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\begin{pmatrix}\cos t&amp;-\sin t\\\sin t&amp;\;\;\cos t\end{pmatrix}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9569f867448c3c98b83eadfebea02cbcb8daaae" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:17.111ex; height:6.176ex;"/></div>
In this case, the action of the matrix on vectors and the multiplication by the complex number <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \cos t+i\sin t}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \cos t+i\sin t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \cos t+i\sin t}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/440a8a3358e2ea3203a610d0a76aad876bd1cbe7" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.45ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> are both the <a href="/wiki/Rotation_(mathematics)" title="Rotation (mathematics)">rotation</a> of the angle <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">t</span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_analysis">Complex analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Complex analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sin1z-cplot.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Sin1z-cplot.svg/220px-Sin1z-cplot.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Sin1z-cplot.svg/330px-Sin1z-cplot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Sin1z-cplot.svg/440px-Sin1z-cplot.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sin1z-cplot.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Domain_coloring" title="Domain coloring">Color wheel graph</a> of <span class="texhtml">sin(1/<i>z</i>)</span>. Black parts inside refer to numbers having large absolute values.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">Complex analysis</a></div>
<p>The study of functions of a complex variable is known as <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a> and has enormous practical use in <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a> as well as in other branches of mathematics. Often, the most natural proofs for statements in <a href="/wiki/Real_analysis" title="Real analysis">real analysis</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> employ techniques from complex analysis (see <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a> for an example). Unlike real functions, which are commonly represented as two-dimensional graphs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_function" title="Complex function">complex functions</a> have four-dimensional graphs and may usefully be illustrated by color-coding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_of_a_function_of_two_variables" title="Graph of a function of two variables">three-dimensional graph</a> to suggest four dimensions, or by animating the complex function's dynamic transformation of the complex plane.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_exponential_and_related_functions">Complex exponential and related functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Complex exponential and related functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The notions of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_series" title="Convergent series">convergent series</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous functions</a> in (real) analysis have natural analogs in complex analysis. A sequence  of complex numbers is said to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convergent_sequence" title="Convergent sequence">converge</a> if and only if its real and imaginary parts do. This is equivalent to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/(%CE%B5,_%CE%B4)-definition_of_limit" title="(ε, δ)-definition of limit">(ε, δ)-definition of limits</a>, where the absolute value of real numbers is replaced by the one of complex numbers. From a more abstract point of view, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, endowed with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_(mathematics)" title="Metric (mathematics)">metric</a>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \operatorname {d} (z_{1},z_{2})=|z_{1}-z_{2}|}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

is a complete <a href="/wiki/Metric_space" title="Metric space">metric space</a>, which notably includes the <a href="/wiki/Triangle_inequality" title="Triangle inequality">triangle inequality</a>
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle |z_{1}+z_{2}|\leq |z_{1}|+|z_{2}|}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

for any two complex numbers <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sub>2</sub></span>.
</p><p>Like in real analysis, this notion of convergence is used to construct a number of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_function" title="Elementary function">elementary functions</a>: the <i><a href="/wiki/Exponential_function" title="Exponential function">exponential function</a></i> <span class="texhtml">exp <i>z</i></span>, also written <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i><sup><i>z</i></sup></span>, is defined as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_series" title="Infinite series">infinite series</a>

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \exp z:=1+z+{\frac {z^{2}}{2\cdot 1}}+{\frac {z^{3}}{3\cdot 2\cdot 1}}+\cdots =\sum _{n=0}^{\infty }{\frac {z^{n}}{n!}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ca8ea97a6ca2dd64faf189a995c6cc80af1cde86" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:48.263ex; height:6.843ex;"/></div>
</p><p>The series defining the real trigonometric functions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_functions" title="Hyperbolic functions">hyperbolic functions</a> sinh and cosh, also carry over to complex arguments without change. For the other trigonometric and hyperbolic functions, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangent_(function)" title="Tangent (function)">tangent</a>, things are slightly more complicated, as the defining series do not converge for all complex values. Therefore, one must define them either in terms of sine, cosine and exponential, or, equivalently, by using the method of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_continuation" title="Analytic continuation">analytic continuation</a>.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler's formula">Euler's formula</a></i> states:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exp(i\varphi )=\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi }" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>exp</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \exp(i\varphi )=\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1578d4fc73aca4efba684f9c66a218c6c871a32a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.594ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
for any real number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">φ</span>, in particular
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exp(i\pi )=-1}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>i</mi>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \exp(i\pi )=-1}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle \exp(i\pi )=-1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eeb48ab5c90a559281efab52cac5c8c552567598" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.565ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>, which is <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_identity" title="Euler's identity">Euler's identity</a>.
Unlike in the situation of real numbers, there is an <a href="/wiki/Infinite_set" title="Infinite set">infinitude</a> of complex solutions <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> of the equation
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exp z=w}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

for any complex number <span class="texhtml"><i>w</i> ≠ 0</span>. It can be shown that any such solution <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> – called <a href="/wiki/Complex_logarithm" title="Complex logarithm">complex logarithm</a> of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">w</span> – satisfies
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \log w=\ln |w|+i\arg w,}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

where arg is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arg_(mathematics)" title="Arg (mathematics)">argument</a> defined <a href="#Polar_form">above</a>, and ln the (real) <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a>. As arg is a <a href="/wiki/Multivalued_function" title="Multivalued function">multivalued function</a>, unique only up to a multiple of <span class="texhtml">2<i>π</i></span>, log is also multivalued. The <a href="/wiki/Principal_value" title="Principal value">principal value</a> of log is often taken by restricting the imaginary part to the <a href="/wiki/Interval_(mathematics)" title="Interval (mathematics)">interval</a> <span class="texhtml">(−<i>π</i>, <i>π</i>]</span>.
</p><p>Complex <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">exponentiation</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i><sup><i>ω</i></sup></span> is defined as
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle z^{\omega }=\exp(\omega \log z),}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>z</mi>

</mrow>

</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle z^{\omega }=\exp(\omega \log z),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bc18e651dc9925a7d4e56de719b70e99638d9cb9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.732ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div>
and is multi-valued, except when <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ω</span> is an integer. For <span class="texhtml"><i>ω</i> = 1 / <i>n</i></span>, for some natural number <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span>, this recovers the non-uniqueness of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n</span>th roots mentioned above.
</p><p>Complex numbers, unlike real numbers, do not in general satisfy the unmodified power and logarithm identities, particularly when naïvely treated as single-valued functions; see <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation#Failure_of_power_and_logarithm_identities" title="Exponentiation">failure of power and logarithm identities</a>. For example, they do not satisfy
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a^{bc}=\left(a^{b}\right)^{c}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

Both sides of the equation are multivalued by the definition of complex exponentiation given here, and the values on the left are a subset of those on the right.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holomorphic_functions">Holomorphic functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Holomorphic functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A function <i>f</i>: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

For example, any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_transformation#Definition_and_first_consequences" title="Linear transformation"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

with complex coefficients <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span>. This map is holomorphic <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i> = 0</span>. The second summand <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle b{\overline {z}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mover>

</semantics>

</p><p>Complex analysis shows some features not apparent in real analysis. For example, any two holomorphic functions <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">g</span> that agree on an arbitrarily small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_subset" title="Open subset">open subset</a> of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> necessarily agree everywhere. <a href="/wiki/Meromorphic_function" title="Meromorphic function">Meromorphic functions</a>, functions that can locally be written as <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>z</i>)/(<i>z</i> − <i>z</i><sub>0</sub>)<sup><i>n</i></sup></span> with a holomorphic function <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span>, still share some of the features of holomorphic functions. Other functions have <a href="/wiki/Essential_singularity" title="Essential singularity">essential singularities</a>, such as <span class="texhtml">sin(1/<i>z</i>)</span> at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 0</span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Complex numbers have applications in many scientific areas, including <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">signal processing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">control theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vibration#Vibration_analysis" title="Vibration">vibration analysis</a>. Some of these applications are described below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry">Geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shapes">Shapes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Shapes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Three <a href="/wiki/Collinearity" title="Collinearity">non-collinear</a> points <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle u,v,w}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>w</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u,v,w}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u,v,w" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4cabca98f60f9ee828adb0d73276eb90eb2ee56" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.189ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> in the plane determine the <a href="/wiki/Shape#Similarity_classes" title="Shape">shape</a> of the triangle <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>w</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">}</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1b64a901f197da5658f531c5b4cbf0ec9c425265" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.514ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. Locating the points in the complex plane, this shape of a triangle may be expressed by complex arithmetic as
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S(u,v,w)={\frac {u-w}{u-v}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>w</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>w</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi>u</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>v</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S(u,v,w)={\frac {u-w}{u-v}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle S(u,v,w)={\frac {u-w}{u-v}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1275fc01560cb752cb3f02f3da8a2087a30cd91" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:19.913ex; height:5.176ex;"/></div>
The shape <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of a triangle will remain the same, when the complex plane is transformed by translation or dilation (by an <a href="/wiki/Affine_transformation" title="Affine transformation">affine transformation</a>), corresponding to the intuitive notion of shape, and describing <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similarity</a>. Thus each triangle <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>u</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>w</mi>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">}</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \{u,v,w\}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1b64a901f197da5658f531c5b4cbf0ec9c425265" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.514ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is in a <a href="/wiki/Shape#Similarity_classes" title="Shape">similarity class</a> of triangles with the same shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fractal_geometry">Fractal geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Fractal geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandelset_hires.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="3121" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mandelset_hires.png/220px-Mandelset_hires.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mandelset_hires.png/330px-Mandelset_hires.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Mandelset_hires.png/440px-Mandelset_hires.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandelset_hires.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Mandelbrot set with the real and imaginary axes labeled.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a> is a popular example of a fractal formed on the complex plane. It is defined by plotting every location <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>c</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle c}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> where iterating the sequence <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f_{c}(z)=z^{2}+c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>z</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{c}(z)=z^{2}+c}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle f_{c}(z)=z^{2}+c}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/191627a3eebdd6608c9b226786defc468b747502" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.071ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> does not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diverge_(stability_theory)" title="Diverge (stability theory)">diverge</a> when <a href="/wiki/Iteration" title="Iteration">iterated</a> infinitely. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Julia_set" title="Julia set">Julia sets</a> have the same rules, except where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>c</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle c}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> remains constant.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Triangles">Triangles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Triangles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Every triangle has a unique <a href="/wiki/Steiner_inellipse" title="Steiner inellipse">Steiner inellipse</a> – an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> inside the triangle and tangent to the midpoints of the three sides of the triangle. The <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">foci</a> of a triangle's Steiner inellipse can be found as follows, according to <a href="/wiki/Marden%27s_theorem" title="Marden's theorem">Marden's theorem</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[54]</a></sup> Denote the triangle's vertices in the complex plane as <span class="texhtml"><i>a</i> = <i>x</i><sub><i>A</i></sub> + <i>y</i><sub><i>A</i></sub><i>i</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>b</i> = <i>x</i><sub><i>B</i></sub> + <i>y</i><sub><i>B</i></sub><i>i</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i> = <i>x</i><sub><i>C</i></sub> + <i>y</i><sub><i>C</i></sub><i>i</i></span>. Write the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_equation" title="Cubic equation">cubic equation</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (x-a)(x-b)(x-c)=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (x-a)(x-b)(x-c)=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(x-a)(x-b)(x-c)=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1856f8d9b149522168258a0bde389d0a53e9c6b1" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:25.433ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, take its derivative, and equate the (quadratic) derivative to zero. Marden's theorem says that the solutions of this equation are the complex numbers denoting the locations of the two foci of the Steiner inellipse.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebraic_number_theory">Algebraic number theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Algebraic number theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pentagon_construct.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Pentagon_construct.gif" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pentagon_construct.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Construction of a regular pentagon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">using straightedge and compass</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As mentioned above, any nonconstant polynomial equation (in complex coefficients) has a solution in <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. <i><a href="/wiki/Argumentum_a_fortiori" title="Argumentum a fortiori">A fortiori</a></i>, the same is true if the equation has rational coefficients. The roots of such equations are called <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number" title="Algebraic number">algebraic numbers</a> – they are a principal object of study in <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number_theory" title="Algebraic number theory">algebraic number theory</a>. Compared to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\overline {\mathbb {Q} }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">Q</mi>
</mrow>
<mo accent="false">¯<!-- ¯ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\overline {\mathbb {Q} }}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\overline {\mathbb {Q} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/377a8814b1ca454c488e409813988dd5dd906148" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.923ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>, the algebraic closure of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {Q} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">Q</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {Q} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {Q} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5909f0b54e4718fa24d5fd34d54189d24a66e9a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, which also contains all algebraic numbers, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> has the advantage of being easily understandable in geometric terms. In this way, algebraic methods can be used to study geometric questions and vice versa. With algebraic methods, more specifically applying the machinery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_theory_(mathematics)" title="Field theory (mathematics)">field theory</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_field" title="Number field">number field</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Root_of_unity" title="Root of unity">roots of unity</a>, it can be shown that it is not possible to construct a regular <a href="/wiki/Nonagon" title="Nonagon">nonagon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions" title="Compass and straightedge constructions">using only compass and straightedge</a> – a purely geometric problem.
</p><p>Another example is the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_integer" title="Gaussian integer">Gaussian integers</a>; that is, numbers of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i> + <i>iy</i></span>, where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">x</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">y</span> are integers, which can be used to classify <a href="/wiki/Fermat%27s_theorem_on_sums_of_two_squares" title="Fermat's theorem on sums of two squares">sums of squares</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analytic_number_theory">Analytic number theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Analytic number theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Analytic_number_theory" title="Analytic number theory">Analytic number theory</a></div>
<p>Analytic number theory studies numbers, often integers or rationals, by taking advantage of the fact that they can be regarded as complex numbers, in which analytic methods can be used. This is done by encoding number-theoretic information in complex-valued functions. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> <span class="texhtml">ζ(<i>s</i>)</span> is related to the distribution of <a href="/wiki/Prime_number" title="Prime number">prime numbers</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improper_integrals">Improper integrals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Improper integrals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In applied fields, complex numbers are often used to compute certain real-valued <a href="/wiki/Improper_integral" title="Improper integral">improper integrals</a>, by means of complex-valued functions. Several methods exist to do this; see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methods_of_contour_integration" title="Methods of contour integration">methods of contour integration</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynamic_equations">Dynamic equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Dynamic equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Differential_equation" title="Differential equation">differential equations</a>, it is common to first find all complex roots <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> of the <a href="/wiki/Linear_differential_equation#Homogeneous_equations_with_constant_coefficients" title="Linear differential equation">characteristic equation</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Linear_differential_equation" title="Linear differential equation">linear differential equation</a> or equation system and then attempt to solve the system in terms of base functions of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>t</i>) = <i>e</i><sup><i>rt</i></sup></span>. Likewise, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Difference_equations" title="Difference equations">difference equations</a>, the complex roots <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> of the characteristic equation of the difference equation system are used, to attempt to solve the system in terms of base functions of the form <span class="texhtml"><i>f</i>(<i>t</i>) = <i>r</i><sup><i>t</i></sup></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_Algebra">Linear Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Linear Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eigendecomposition_of_a_matrix" title="Eigendecomposition of a matrix">Eigendecomposition</a> is a useful tool for computing matrix powers and <a href="/wiki/Matrix_exponential" title="Matrix exponential">matrix exponentials</a>. However, it often requires the use of complex numbers, even if the matrix is real (for example, a <a href="/wiki/Rotation_matrix" title="Rotation matrix">rotation matrix</a>).
</p><p>Complex numbers often generalize concepts originally conceived in the real numbers. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Conjugate_transpose" title="Conjugate transpose">conjugate transpose</a> generalizes the <a href="/wiki/Transpose" title="Transpose">transpose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermitian_matrix" title="Hermitian matrix">hermitian matrices</a> generalize <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_matrix" title="Symmetric matrix">symmetric matrices</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unitary_matrix" title="Unitary matrix">unitary matrices</a> generalize <a href="/wiki/Orthogonal_matrix" title="Orthogonal matrix">orthogonal matrices</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_applied_mathematics">In applied mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: In applied mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_theory">Control theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Control theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane#Use_in_control_theory" title="Complex plane">Complex plane § Use in control theory</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">control theory</a>, systems are often transformed from the <a href="/wiki/Time_domain" title="Time domain">time domain</a> to the complex <a href="/wiki/Frequency_domain" title="Frequency domain">frequency domain</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Laplace_transform" title="Laplace transform">Laplace transform</a>. The system's <a href="/wiki/Zeros_and_poles" title="Zeros and poles">zeros and poles</a> are then analyzed in the <i>complex plane</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Root_locus" title="Root locus">root locus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyquist_plot" title="Nyquist plot">Nyquist plot</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nichols_plot" title="Nichols plot">Nichols plot</a> techniques all make use of the complex plane.
</p><p>In the root locus method, it is important whether zeros and poles are in the left or right half planes, that is, have real part greater than or less than zero. If a linear, time-invariant (LTI) system has poles that are
</p>
<ul><li>in the right half plane, it will be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unstable" title="Unstable">unstable</a>,</li>
<li>all in the left half plane, it will be <a href="/wiki/BIBO_stability" title="BIBO stability">stable</a>,</li>
<li>on the imaginary axis, it will have <a href="/wiki/Marginal_stability" title="Marginal stability">marginal stability</a>.</li></ul>
<p>If a system has zeros in the right half plane, it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonminimum_phase" title="Nonminimum phase">nonminimum phase</a> system.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Signal_analysis">Signal analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Signal analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Complex numbers are used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_analysis" title="Signal analysis">signal analysis</a> and other fields for a convenient description for periodically varying signals. For given real functions representing actual physical quantities, often in terms of sines and cosines, corresponding complex functions are considered of which the real parts are the original quantities. For a <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a> of a given <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>, the absolute value <span class="texhtml">|<i>z</i>|</span> of the corresponding <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Argument_(complex_analysis)" title="Argument (complex analysis)">argument</a> <span class="texhtml">arg <i>z</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a>.
</p><p>If <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a> is employed to write a given real-valued signal as a sum of periodic functions, these periodic functions are often written as complex-valued functions of the form
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x(t)=\operatorname {Re} \{X(t)\}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>Re</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">{</mo>

</semantics>

</p><p>and
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle X(t)=Ae^{i\omega t}=ae^{i\phi }e^{i\omega t}=ae^{i(\omega t+\phi )}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X(t)=Ae^{i\omega t}=ae^{i\phi }e^{i\omega t}=ae^{i(\omega t+\phi )}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle X(t)=Ae^{i\omega t}=ae^{i\phi }e^{i\omega t}=ae^{i(\omega t+\phi )}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/50e065a79a4803b81d5dd1e938da8cfa8c8d8087" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:35.025ex; height:3.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>where ω represents the <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">angular frequency</a> and the complex number <i>A</i> encodes the phase and amplitude as explained above.
</p><p>This use is also extended into <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal_processing" title="Digital signal processing">digital signal processing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Digital_image_processing" title="Digital image processing">digital image processing</a>, which utilize digital versions of Fourier analysis (and <a href="/wiki/Wavelet" title="Wavelet">wavelet</a> analysis) to transmit, <a href="/wiki/Data_compression" title="Data compression">compress</a>, restore, and otherwise process <a href="/wiki/Digital_data" title="Digital data">digital</a> <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">audio</a> signals, still images, and <a href="/wiki/Video" title="Video">video</a> signals.
</p><p>Another example, relevant to the two side bands of <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">amplitude modulation</a> of AM radio, is:
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\cos((\omega +\alpha )t)+\cos \left((\omega -\alpha )t\right)&amp;=\operatorname {Re} \left(e^{i(\omega +\alpha )t}+e^{i(\omega -\alpha )t}\right)\\&amp;=\operatorname {Re} \left(\left(e^{i\alpha t}+e^{-i\alpha t}\right)\cdot e^{i\omega t}\right)\\&amp;=\operatorname {Re} \left(2\cos(\alpha t)\cdot e^{i\omega t}\right)\\&amp;=2\cos(\alpha t)\cdot \operatorname {Re} \left(e^{i\omega t}\right)\\&amp;=2\cos(\alpha t)\cdot \cos \left(\omega t\right).\end{aligned}}}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_physics">In physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: In physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetism_and_electrical_engineering">Electromagnetism and electrical engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Electromagnetism and electrical engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">Alternating current</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a> is used to analyze varying <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">currents</a>. The treatment of <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">resistors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">inductors</a> can then be unified by introducing imaginary, frequency-dependent resistances for the latter two and combining all three in a single complex number called the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_impedance" title="Electrical impedance">impedance</a>. This approach is called <a href="/wiki/Phasor" title="Phasor">phasor</a> calculus.
</p><p>In electrical engineering, the imaginary unit is denoted by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">j</span>, to avoid confusion with <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">I</span>, which is generally in use to denote <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>, or, more particularly, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span>, which is generally in use to denote instantaneous electric current.
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> in an AC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" title="Electric circuit">circuit</a> is oscillating, it can be represented as
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V(t)=V_{0}e^{j\omega t}=V_{0}\left(\cos \omega t+j\sin \omega t\right),}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle V(t)=V_{0}e^{j\omega t}=V_{0}\left(\cos \omega t+j\sin \omega t\right),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/db52b30a48d1206b576a033d782bf35752bb248f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:37.789ex; height:3.176ex;"/></div>
</p><p>To obtain the measurable quantity, the real part is taken:
</p><p><div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v(t)=\operatorname {Re} (V)=\operatorname {Re} \left[V_{0}e^{j\omega t}\right]=V_{0}\cos \omega t.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle v(t)=\operatorname {Re} (V)=\operatorname {Re} \left[V_{0}e^{j\omega t}\right]=V_{0}\cos \omega t.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b9078e78decc9fdf5d57a237bbf756b9cc438a0" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:39.34ex; height:3.343ex;"/></div>
</p><p>The complex-valued signal <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i>(<i>t</i>)</span> is called the <a href="/wiki/Analytic_signal" title="Analytic signal">analytic</a> representation of the real-valued, measurable signal <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i>(<i>t</i>)</span>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluid_dynamics">Fluid dynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Fluid dynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, complex functions are used to describe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potential_flow_in_two_dimensions" title="Potential flow in two dimensions">potential flow in two dimensions</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanics">Quantum mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The complex number field is intrinsic to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_formulations_of_quantum_mechanics" title="Mathematical formulations of quantum mechanics">mathematical formulations of quantum mechanics</a>, where complex <a href="/wiki/Hilbert_space" title="Hilbert space">Hilbert spaces</a> provide the context for one such formulation that is convenient and perhaps most standard. The original foundation formulas of quantum mechanics – the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> and Heisenberg's <a href="/wiki/Matrix_mechanics" title="Matrix mechanics">matrix mechanics</a> – make use of complex numbers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativity">Relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, some formulas for the metric on <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> become simpler if one takes the time component of the spacetime continuum to be imaginary. (This approach is no longer standard in classical relativity, but is <a href="/wiki/Wick_rotation" title="Wick rotation">used in an essential way</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>.) Complex numbers are essential to <a href="/wiki/Spinor" title="Spinor">spinors</a>, which are a generalization of the <a href="/wiki/Tensor" title="Tensor">tensors</a> used in relativity.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations_and_related_notions">Generalizations and related notions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Complex_number&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Generalizations and related notions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg/230px-Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg/345px-Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg/460px-Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cayley_Q8_quaternion_multiplication_graph.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cayley Q8 quaternion graph showing cycles of multiplication by <span style="color: red;"><b>i</b></span>, <span style="color:green;"><b>j</b></span> and <span style="color:blue"><b>k</b></span></div></div></div>
<p>The process of extending the field <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/786849c765da7a84dbc3cce43e96aad58a5868dc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of reals to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">C</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {C} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9add4085095b9b6d28d045fd9c92c2c09f549a7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is known as the <a href="/wiki/Cayley%E2%80%93Dickson_construction" title="Cayley–Dickson construction">Cayley–Dickson construction</a>. It can be carried further to higher dimensions, yielding the <a href="/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {H} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">H</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {H} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {H} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e050965453c42bcc6bd544546703c836bdafeac9" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <a href="/wiki/Octonion" title="Octonion">octonions</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {O} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">O</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {O} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {O} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1ed2664a4fe515e6fbed25a7193ce663b82920c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> which (as a real vector space) are of dimension 4 and 8, respectively.
In this context the complex numbers have been called the <b>binarions</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Just as by applying the construction to reals the property of <a href="/wiki/Ordered_field" title="Ordered field">ordering</a> is lost, properties familiar from real and complex numbers vanish with each extension. The <a href="/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a> lose commutativity, that is, <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i>·<i>y</i> ≠ <i>y</i>·<i>x</i></span> for some quaternions <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i>, <i>y</i></span>, and the multiplication of <a href="/wiki/Octonion" title="Octonion">octonions</a>, additionally to not being commutative, fails to be associative: <span class="texhtml">(<i>x</i>·<i>y</i>)·<i>z</i> ≠ <i>x</i>·(<i>y</i>·<i>z</i>)</span> for some octonions <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i></span>.
</p><p>Reals, complex numbers, quaternions and octonions are all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normed_division_algebra" title="Normed division algebra">normed division algebras</a> over <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/786849c765da7a84dbc3cce43e96aad58a5868dc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. By <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurwitz%27s_theorem_(normed_division_algebras)" title="Hurwitz's theorem (normed division algebras)">Hurwitz's theorem</a> they are the only ones; the <a href="/wiki/Sedenion" title="Sedenion">sedenions</a>, the next step in the Cayley–Dickson construction, fail to have this structure.
</p><p>The Cayley–Dickson construction is closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Regular_representation" title="Regular representation">regular representation</a> of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbb {C} ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6ff6a3dc2982018ff20f1d2c927afc74a217be6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.325ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> thought of as an <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

This means the following: the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hypercomplex_number" title="Hypercomplex number">Hypercomplex numbers</a> also generalize <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

There are no other nontrivial ways of completing <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {Q} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
